 CHAPTER 1

The salt came for me first. It always does. It’s a thief, you see. A slow, patient thief that doesn’t bother with your purse or your silver. It takes the moisture from your skin, the softness from your hair, the give from your leather boots until they stand on their own like little sentinels by the door. It powders the windowsills, crusts the corners of your eyes in the morning, and leaves a perpetual, desiccating kiss on your lips. Gran always said the salt was the memory of the sea, trying to get back in. I think it’s the sea’s revenge for us leaving it.

I was on my knees, not praying, but scrubbing. The flagstones of the kitchen floor were the colour of old bone, and the brine that seeped up through the cracks in dry weather left a white, filigree pattern, like a map of a country no one wanted to visit. My knuckles were raw. Aunt Marva believed in cleanliness as a moral imperative, a bulwark against the chaos of the outer world and the inner mind. “A dirty floor is a dirty soul, Lyra,” she’d say, her voice the dry rustle of reeds. “Scrub until you see your own reflection, then scrub until you don’t.”

I couldn’t see my reflection. I saw the ghost of my mother’s face instead, swimming in the wet stone. A trick of the light, a quirk of memory. She had hair like frayed rope, sun-bleached and thick, and eyes the grey-green of a winter wave. In my memory, she is always laughing, a sound that started low in her belly and erupted, uncontainable. Aunt Marva said that laugh was what called the trouble. Too much joy, she said, is a beacon.

The back door, warped by damp and salt, blew open with a gasp. Not a slam—the air here is too heavy for slamming—just a weary exhalation that let in the smell of the marsh: peat, decaying reed, the sweet-sour tang of samphire, and beneath it all, the iron breath of the distant sea. A figure filled the doorway, blotting out the flat, grey light.

“She’s asking for you.”

My cousin, Kestrel. Eighteen to my seventeen, but he seemed carved from something older, something that had weathered. His hands were stained with engine grease and peat, his trousers caked to the knee in black mud. He had our mother’s eyes, but where hers had held the sea’s movement, his were still, like water in a deep well.

“Asking or summoning?” I didn’t stop scrubbing. The rhythm of it—the scrape of bristle on stone, the swish of water—was a kind of incantation. A way to keep my own thoughts at bay.

“Does it matter?” He came in, his boots leaving dark prints on my clean, wet stone. A provocation. He nodded at the bucket. “You’re wasting your time. The salt comes back before the water’s even dry.”

“So does everything else,” I said. “Tide comes in, tide goes out. Dirt rises. People make demands.”

He almost smiled. It was a rare, fleeting thing, like a crack in a wall. “Philosophy while you scrub. Gran would approve. The demand is now, Lyra. The tide’s on the turn.”

I sat back on my heels, wiping my forehead with the back of my wrist. The salt there, mixed with sweat, stung. “What does she want?”

“The story,” he said simply. “The Fisherwife’s Tide.”

A cold knot tightened in my stomach, different from the usual dull ache of this place. That story wasn’t for daytime. It wasn’t for dry throats and scrubbed floors. It was a night-story, a fire-story, told when the wind howled in from the east and the marsh water went black as oil. It was Gran’s story. Her voice was the only vessel that could carry it.

“Why now?”

Kestrel shrugged, a slow, heavy movement. “You know her. The reasons come after the wanting.”

I dumped the scrub brush into the bucket. The water was a cloudy grey. My reflection, when it settled, was a smudge of pale skin and dark, unruly hair. I didn’t look like my mother. I looked like a girl trying to hold a coastline that was determined to erode.

Gran’s room was at the front of the cottage, facing the sea you could hear but never quite see, hidden behind the endless, rippling skin of the marsh and the dunes. It was a room of relics. On the mantelpiece, a brass compass that no longer pointed north but spun in lazy, unpredictable circles. On the wall, a chart of the coastline, hand-drawn on vellum so old it had grown translucent, the landmasses blurred like fading bruises. The air smelled of camphor, dried lavender, and the faint, ever-present salt.

She was propped in a high-backed chair, a quilt of a thousand faded patches over her legs. She was small, my gran, a bundle of bird-bones and sharp intelligence. Her hair, once as black as marsh-peat, was a wild cloud of white. She didn’t look at me when I entered, but at the window, as if watching for a ship on a horizon that wasn’t there.

“You took your time,” she said. Her voice wasn’t reed-rustle like Marva’s. It was pebbles rolling in a surge, a deep, granular sound.

“The floor,” I said.

“Floors can wait. Memory can’t.” She finally turned her head. Her eyes were the colour of flint, and just as hard. “Sit. There, on the stool.”

I perched on the low wooden stool at her feet. The light was failing, bleeding out of the flat sky. Kestrel leaned in the doorway, a silent spectator.

“You know the story,” Gran stated.

“I’ve heard it.”

“Hearing isn’t knowing. Knowing is holding it in your blood. Today, you start knowing.” She drew a long, rattling breath. “This isn’t a fairy story. It’s a tide-table. A warning buoy. You listen with your skin.”

She closed her eyes, and when she began, her voice changed. It lost its granular edge, becoming smoother, wider, a channel opening up.

“Before the maps, before the breakwaters and the measured depths, the women here knew the water’s mind. They read the colour of the waves, the taste of the wind, the way the gulls circled not for food, but for news. They were the Fisherwives. They didn’t just catch fish; they negotiated with the deep. They knew when to go out and, more importantly, when to call everyone back.”

I’d heard this part. The Fisherwives with their nets mended with songs, their baskets that always held one more fish than possible, their ability to smell a storm three days off. The power in it was practical, domestic, fierce.

“The greatest of them,” Gran continued, her voice dropping, “was a woman without a name in any ledger. She had hair like the heart of a fire and a laugh that could calm a squall. She married a man from the inland hills, a man who loved the solidity of stone and hated the sea’s fickleness. He built her a house of good, grey stone, right on the shingle bank. A fortress, he said. Against the tide.”

I felt a chill that had nothing to do with the damp room. This was the turn. The part where the air always grew still.

“She bore him three daughters. Three. Each birth, she went down to the water’s edge and let the waves take the pain, take the blood. She gave the sea its tithe. But her husband saw only the mess, the wildness. He wanted order. He wanted the sea kept at bay. He built a wall. A high, strong wall between his stone house and the water.”

Gran’s hands, resting on the quilt, clenched. The knuckles were islands of white.

“The Fisherwife stood at her window, her daughters at her skirts, and watched her horizon become a line of cut stone. She stopped laughing. The colour left her hair. She began to forget the songs for mending nets. One night, during the great autumn gale, the sea sang a note so low it was felt, not heard. A calling. She walked out of the stone house, through the door her husband had locked, through the wall he had built. She walked into the waves, her daughters’ hands in hers.”

She opened her eyes. They were full of a terrible, clear light.
“And?” I whispered, though I knew.
“And the sea took its due. Not as tithe, but as claim. The husband woke to an empty house, the door swinging open to a shore scoured clean of footprints. All that was left was the sound, they say. The echo of a laugh, tangled in the wind and the spray. Some say she drowned them all. Some say she saved them. The story doesn’t say. The story just is. A Fisherwife’s tide always comes in. You can build your wall, but the water remembers where the land used to be.”

The room was silent. The story hung in the air, a new kind of salt.

Gran leaned forward, her gaze pinning me to the stool. “Your mother,” she said, each word a stone dropped into a deep pool, “had that laugh.”

The world tilted. The cold knot in my stomach turned to ice, then to a sharp, splintering ache. I felt Kestrel go rigid in the doorway.

“She walked into the sea,” I said, my voice not my own. It was a flat, dead thing. I’d been told she’d slipped. An accident. A tragic misstep on the rocks.

“She answered a call,” Gran corrected, her voice back to flint. “Seven years ago tonight. The same tide. The same gale.”

I stood up, the stool scraping harshly on the floorboards. “Why are you telling me this now?”

“Because you’re forgetting her,” Gran said, not unkindly, but with a brutal finality. “You scrub her out of the floors. You let Marva bake her out of the bread with rules and silence. You are building a very nice, very strong wall, Lyra. And the tide is on the turn.”

I turned and walked out. Past Kestrel, whose face was a mask of conflicted storm. Down the narrow hall, through the kitchen where my bucket of grey water sat accusingly. I didn’t stop until I was outside, the marsh wind slapping me awake.

The sky was the colour of a fresh bruise, yellow and purple at the edges. The air thrummed with the pressure of the coming weather. I walked, not towards anything, just away. The path was a ribbon of packed mud and crushed shell through the reeds. The sound of the sea, which was usually a distant whisper, was now a clear, insistent roar.

I came to the place where the path forked. One way led back to the village, to lights and smoke and pretended safety. The other led to the Shingle Bank, and the ruins of a grey stone house.

I stood at the fork, my raw hands clenched at my sides. The salt air filled my lungs, the thief taking my breath now, too. I thought of my mother’s laugh, a sound I realized I could barely remember. I thought of a woman walking into the waves, holding her daughters’ hands.

And for the first time, I didn’t see a tragedy. I saw a choice.

A drop of rain, heavy and cold, hit my cheek. Then another.

The tide was on the turn.

I took the path to the Shingle Bank.

 CHAPTER 2

The rain didn’t fall so much as it was unleashed. It came in a sweeping, horizontal curtain, driven by a wind that had found its purpose. It wasn’t the gentle, soaking rain of the marsh; this was the sea’s rain, sharp with salt, needling its way through my wool jacket as if the fabric were gauze. The reeds around me thrashed and hissed, a thousand green tongues telling me to go back.

I didn’t go back. The path became a stream of liquid peat, sucking at my boots. My hair, whipped from its plait, lashed my face. Gran’s words were a drumbeat in my head, syncopated with the pounding of my heart. She walked into the waves. She answered a call. You are building a wall.

The Shingle Bank was a fist of land the sea hadn’t quite decided to claim or relinquish. It was a landscape of negotiation: great, smooth grey stones tumbled by ancient tides, banks of pebbles that chattered like teeth when the water pulled back, and stubborn, salt-burned grass that grew in defiant tufts. And there, at the highest point, its back to the mainland like a child facing a punishment, were the ruins.

The stone house wasn’t a romantic collapse. It was a blunt, stubborn ruin. Two walls still stood, meeting at a corner, the mortar between the stones long since scoured away so they looked like giant, rotten teeth. The other walls were heaps of rubble, colonized by thrift and sea campion. The roof was a memory. From the doorway—a dark, empty rectangle—you could look straight out at the raging, white-capped sea.

This was the fortress. The wall against the tide.

I stood in what was left of the doorway, the rain slicing through the open space. The wind howled through the gaps in the stone, a low, mournful note. The echo of a laugh, Gran had said. I heard only the dirge of the storm.

Why had I come? To prove Gran wrong? To see the stage for the story and confirm it was just that—a story, stones and wind? To feel closer to a mother whose shape was blurring in my mind, replaced by the mythic figure of a Fisherwife?

I stepped inside. The floor was a carpet of crushed shell, sand, and sheep droppings. In the corner of the two standing walls, someone had made a recent, pathetic attempt at a fire—a circle of blackened stones, a few charred sticks of driftwood. Kestrel, probably. He came out here to smoke stolen cigarettes and be away from Aunt Marva’s watchful silence.

I walked to the inner face of the wall that faced the sea. The stone was smoother here, sheltered from the worst of the weather. And there, I saw them.

Faint, but undeniable. Scratched into the stone, not with a tool, but with something softer, repeated over and over. A child’s drawings. My breath caught.

A wobbly circle with lines for rays. A sun.
A stick figure with a triangular skirt.
A bigger stick figure holding the smaller one’s hand.
And beside them, a series of rolling, connected humps. The sea.

I reached out. The stone was cold, but the grooves, worn shallow by time, felt warm under my fingertips. A memory, not my own, flickered—a small hand, mine, clutching a piece of chalky white shell, dragging it across the stone, giggling. A larger hand covering mine, guiding it. Like this, Lyra. Make the waves big and proud.

I snatched my hand back as if burned. I had been here. With her. We had drawn on this wall. This was not a story from a thousand years ago. This was my history, etched in granite.

The wind rose to a shriek, and with it came a new sound—a deep, resonant boom that vibrated up through the soles of my boots. The sea, throwing itself against the foundations of the Bank. I turned to the doorway.

And I saw her.

Not a ghost. Not a shimmering apparition. A woman, standing ankle-deep in the churning foam where the shingle met the water, about fifty yards from the ruin. She was silhouetted against the bruised sky and the white fury of the waves, her dress—a simple, dark shift—plastered to her body by the wind and rain. Her hair was a wild, streaming banner. She stood perfectly still, facing the horizon.

My blood turned to ice, then to fire. Mother.

The word was a silent scream in my throat. I took a step forward, out of the ruin’s scant shelter, the rain immediately blinding me. I wiped my eyes, my heart hammering against my ribs.

“Hello?” I cried, but the wind stole the word, tore it to pieces.

She didn’t turn. She took a step forward, into the surf. Then another. The water was at her knees.

Every cell in my body screamed. This was the story. It was happening again. Was I seeing a memory etched in the air itself? Was Gran’s telling so powerful it had conjured a vision?

“Stop!” I stumbled forward, my boots slipping on the wet stones. I fell, my knees hitting the shingle with a painful crunch. I scrambled up, salt water soaking my trousers.

When I looked again, she was deeper. The water swirled around her thighs. She was going to walk in. She was going to answer the call.

I ran. Not with thought, but with a primal, terrified instinct. This was not a myth. This was a woman. This was my story, now, and I would not be the daughter left in the empty stone house.

The shingle shifted treacherously beneath me. A wave, larger than the others, crashed and surged up the bank, a cold, grasping hand that wrapped around my calves and tried to pull me down. I fought through it, the water dragging at me, heavy as regret.

“Wait!” I was closer now, close enough to see the details. The frayed hem of the dress. The way her shoulders were set, not in despair, but in a kind of fierce acceptance.

She took another step. The water was at her waist.

I plunged into the surf. The cold was a shock that stole my breath. It was like plunging into liquid stone. The undertow pulled, a hungry, insistent force. “Stop! Please!”

She paused. Her head tilted, as if listening to a voice just beneath the wind’s roar.

Then, she turned.

It wasn’t my mother.

The face that looked back at me was older, etched not by laughter but by a weather of grief and salt. The eyes were not grey-green, but a pale, washed-out blue, like sky reflected in a puddle. It was a face I knew from the village. Maeve. Maeve who worked the oyster beds, whose son had been lost with the Starling three winters ago. Maeve, who was said to walk the shores at night, listening for bells.

The recognition was a second, colder wave. This wasn’t a ghost from my past. This was a living woman, walking into her future.

Our eyes locked across the churning, foam-flecked distance. In her gaze, I saw no madness, only a profound, exhausted resolve. She looked at me as if from a great distance, as if I were the one who was indistinct, a figure from a forgotten shore.

She gave me the faintest nod. An acknowledgement. A farewell.

Then she turned back to the horizon, and with a strength that seemed impossible against the drag of the sea, she took another step forward.

“MAEVE!” I screamed, the name ripped raw from my throat.

I lunged, throwing myself through the water. My fingers brushed the sodden wool of her sleeve, clutched, held. The force of the next wave broke over us both, a ton of freezing, green water that slammed us down into the roaring dark.

The world vanished into chaos. There was no up, no down, only a tumbling, deafening violence. My grip was torn away. I was a rag in a giant’s fist, thrown, spun, my mouth full of salt and terror. My shoulder slammed into something hard—the seabed, a rock—and a bolt of white pain shot through me.

The Fisherwife’s tide, I thought, with a strange, clear detachment. It comes in.

Then, a hand. Not the sea’s hand. A human hand, strong and calloused, closed around my forearm with a grip that could crush bone. It hauled, against the will of the ocean itself.

I broke the surface, choking, retching seawater. I was being dragged backwards, my boots scraping over shingle. I caught a glimpse of Maeve, a dark shape being pulled further out, a wave cresting over her head.

“No! Get her!” I spluttered, fighting the grip.

“Quiet.” The voice was a guttural command in my ear. Kestrel.

He had me under the arms now, heaving me out of the surf like a sodden sack. I thrashed, my eyes fixed on the spot where Maeve had disappeared. “She’s out there! Kestrel, she’s—”

“I see her.” His voice was tight, grim. He dumped me on the higher shingle, beyond the reach of the surging waves. “Stay.”

He turned and ran along the bank, not into the water, but towards a point of rock that jutted out like a finger. He moved with a sure-footed grace I never possessed, leaping from stone to stone. I pushed myself up on trembling arms, my shoulder screaming in protest. I saw him reach the point, scan the raging water, then dive, not in an arc, but in a straight, knife-sharp plunge that took him deep beneath the chaos of the surface.

Time stopped. The wind roared. The sea boomed. I knelt on the stones, shivering violently, salt water streaming from my hair and clothes, and waited.

A lifetime later, two heads broke the surface, close to the rocks. Kestrel, one arm locked across Maeve’s chest, fighting the swell to get her to the relative shelter of the point. I stumbled to my feet and ran towards them, skirting the water’s edge.

By the time I reached them, Kestrel had Maeve on a flat shelf of rock. She was coughing, vomiting seawater, her body convulsing with cold and shock. Alive.

Kestrel looked up at me, his face a mask of water and fury. “What in the name of the deep were you doing?”

“She was just… standing there… then she walked in…” My teeth were chattering so hard I could barely speak.

He stripped off his own oilskin jacket and wrapped it around Maeve, then turned his fury back on me. “And you decided to go for a swim? You could have drowned. You both could have drowned!”

“I thought…” I hugged my injured arm to my chest. “I thought she was someone else.”

The anger drained from his face, replaced by a weary understanding. He looked from me to the ruins of the stone house, then back to the sea. “Gran told you the story.”

It wasn’t a question. I just nodded.

He sighed, a sound lost in the wind. “Help me get her up. We need to get her warm. And you.” He looked at my shivering form, my probably-dislocated shoulder. “You need to explain to Aunt Marva why you look like a drowned selkie who’s been in a fight.”

Between us, we got Maeve to her feet. She was limp, her eyes closed, muttering something that sounded like, “The bells… too late…”

As we half-carried, half-dragged her back along the path, away from the Shingle Bank, I looked over my shoulder one last time. The ruins were a dark smudge against the storm. The sea raged, unabated. It didn’t care about our dramas. It just was. A constant, hungry presence.

But I had touched the wall with the drawings. I had seen a woman walk into the waves. I had felt the cold, claiming grip of the tide.

Gran was right. I had been building a wall. And tonight, the first crack had appeared, not in the stone, but in me. Let in by memory, by salt, by a story that was suddenly, terrifyingly, my own.

 CHAPTER 3

Aunt Marva did not shout. Her disapproval was a silent, physical force, like a change in atmospheric pressure. It filled the steaming kitchen, thicker than the scent of the thyme and comfrey tea she was brewing. It pressed against my bruised shoulder, which she had wordlessly popped back into its socket with a swift, brutal twist that left me seeing stars. It condensed on the windows, already fogged from the kettle.

I sat at the scrubbed table, wrapped in two rough wool blankets, a third around my hair like a shawl. My clothes were in a sopping heap by the door. Maeve was in the front room by the fire, bundled and sedated with a draught of something strong and herbal. Kestrel was outside, seeing to the boat, or just staying out of the line of fire.

Marva placed a chipped mug of tea in front of me. The liquid was the colour of mud, steaming ominously. “Drink.”

I obeyed. It tasted of earth and bitterness, with an underlying sweetness that did nothing to mask the medicine. It hit my stomach and sent a wave of artificial warmth through my core.

She sat opposite me, her hands folded on the table. They were capable hands, red-knuckled, nails short and clean. Hands that built walls of order, stone by stone. “Explain.”

I took another sip of the dreadful tea. “I went to the Shingle Bank.”

“I ascertained that much. The why eludes me.”

“Gran told me a story.”

A flicker in her eyes. A tiny crack in the marble. “Your grandmother traffics in old sorrows. She mistakes them for wisdom.”

“It was about the Fisherwife. The one who walked into the sea.”

“A parable for foolish women who listen to the wind instead of their families.” Her voice was flat. “It has nothing to do with you.”

“It has everything to do with me!” The words burst out, fueled by the tea and the lingering adrenaline. “She told me Mother… that she didn’t slip. That she walked in. On purpose. On the same tide, seven years ago.”

Marva’s face didn’t change, but a muscle in her jaw tightened. She looked, for a moment, like a cliff face weathering a gale. “Your mother,” she said carefully, “was a creature of impulse. Of feeling. The sea was a feeling to her. A grand, romantic feeling. That night… the feeling was too grand. That is all.”

“That’s not all! Gran said she answered a call. Like the Fisherwife.”

“And you went to the Bank to answer it yourself?” Her question was a scalpel. “To follow her? Is that what you were doing when Kestrel found you trying to become a permanent feature of the seabed?”

“No! I saw Maeve. I thought…” I trailed off, the absurdity of it crashing down. “I thought it was her. For a second, I really did.”

Marva was silent for a long time. She looked at the pile of my wet clothes, at the steam rising from my mug, at anything but me. When she spoke again, her voice was lower, stripped of its usual crispness. It was the voice she used for bad news, for dead lambs, for broken things.

“Your mother,” she said, “heard music no one else could hear. She saw colours in the fog. She believed the seals could talk and that the northern lights were the souls of Fisherwives dancing. It made her beautiful. It made her impossible to hold onto. Your father tried. This house tried. I tried.” She finally met my eyes. “The world is not made for people who live in stories, Lyra. It breaks them. Or it drowns them.”

“So you just… stopped talking about her? You let me think it was an accident? You let me forget her laugh?” My voice trembled.

“I let you live,” Marva said fiercely. “I built a wall between you and that… that pull. The pull that took her. Stories are doors, child. And some doors should remain shut.”

“You don’t get to decide that for me.”

“I do,” she said, standing up, the chair legs scraping on the stone floor. “As long as you are under this roof, eating the food I grow and preserve, walking on the floors I insist you scrub, you will live in the real world. Not in your grandmother’s haunted memories, not in your mother’s fatal poetry. You will live here.” She brought her hand down flat on the table. The mugs jumped. “Is that understood?”

It was an ultimatum. A line drawn in the salt of the floor. The wall, fully revealed.

I understood then that Aunt Marva was also a Fisherwife of a kind. Not one who negotiated with the deep, but one who fought it, day after day, with routine and discipline and silence. Her nets were her routines, her songs were her rules, and her catch was our survival. She was trying to haul me, kicking and screaming, onto dry land.

I looked down into my tea. The warmth it provided felt false, thin. The real cold was inside, in the new, hollow place where the old, simple story of my mother had been.

“I understand,” I whispered.

She nodded, a single, sharp dip of her chin. The pressure in the room eased, but it didn’t dissipate. It just settled, a permanent front. “Good. Finish your tea. Then go to bed. You will still be expected to do your chores tomorrow. The world doesn’t stop for epiphanies.”

She turned to the sink, her back a rigid declaration of the conversation’s end.

I sat there, the wool blankets itching my skin. I thought of the drawings on the wall. Make the waves big and proud. I thought of Maeve’s exhausted, resolved face as she turned towards the horizon. I thought of my mother, not as a tragic figure or a mythic Fisherwife, but as a woman who heard music. Who saw colours.

Gran’s voice echoed. The tide is on the turn.

Upstairs, in the room I shared with the ghosts of my own childhood, I didn’t sleep. I lay in the narrow bed, listening to the house groan in the wind, to the distant, relentless boom of the sea. My shoulder throbbed. My mind churned.

Just before dawn, when the grey light was a thin smear on the window, I heard it. Faint, woven into the fabric of the wind.

Music.

Not a tune. Not anything you could whistle. It was a low, rhythmic thrum, like a giant cello string being plucked deep under the water. A vibration more than a sound. It was in the stones of the house, in the bones of my bed. It was the feeling you get before a thunderclap, stretched out into a melody that had no beginning or end.

I sat bolt upright, my heart hammering. I looked at Kestrel’s empty bed across the room. He was still out, or downstairs.

I crept to the window. The marsh was a sheet of leaden water and shifting mist. The music—the call—seemed to come from everywhere and nowhere. It wasn’t beautiful. It was compelling. It was a question asked in a language of pressure and pulse.

This is what she heard.

The realization was a certainty. This was the colour in the fog, the seal’s talk, the music no one else could hear. It was real. And it was here.

A soft knock at my door made me jump. It swung open before I could answer. Kestrel stood there, still dressed in his damp, salt-stiffened clothes from the night before. He looked exhausted, but his eyes were alert.

“You’re awake,” he said.

“You hear it too,” I breathed.

He didn’t pretend not to understand. He just nodded, once. “Sometimes. More, lately.” He stepped into the room, closing the door softly behind him. “Maeve’s awake. She’s… confused. But she asked for you.”

“For me? Why?”

“She says she has a message. For the daughter of the woman with the laugh.”

The world narrowed to a tunnel. The thrumming in the air seemed to grow louder, synchronizing with my heartbeat. “What message?”

“She wouldn’t tell me. She’ll only say it to you.” He studied my face. “You don’t have to go. Marva would lose her mind. After last night…”

He was giving me an out. A chance to stay behind my aunt’s wall.

I looked past him, out the window, towards the invisible sea. I thought of the choice my mother made. I thought of the wall I’d been building, stone by dutiful stone. I thought of the other wall, the one of grey stone on the Shingle Bank, that had failed to keep the water out.

The tide was on the turn. A door was opening.

“Take me to her,” I said.

---

 Chapter 4

 CHAPTER 4

The memory of the sea doesn’t just take. It gives back, too. It gives you ghosts. Not the wispy, sorrowful kind from stories, but the solid, salt-cured kind. The kind that remember the shape of teeth, the heft of a harpoon, the cold, crushing embrace of the deep. They are echoes with intention. And in the Veil, where the past bleeds through like brine through cracked stone, the echoes are hungry.

Elara and I moved through a landscape of memory. The path wasn’t dirt or rock, but a substance like hardened mist, shot through with veins of phosphorescent green. It gave underfoot like wet sand, but left no imprint. The air was thick and silent, a silence so complete it became a noise of its own—a high, thin ringing in the ears. The call that had drawn me here was gone, swallowed by this deeper quiet. We were in the throat of the place.

“It feels like a held breath,” Elara whispered. Her voice was too loud, a sacrilege.

I nodded, my hand closed around the key in my pocket. It was warm, not with the heat of life, but with a low, persistent hum, like a stone left in the sun. It was the only thing that felt real.

We saw the ghosts first as a distortion in the air, a shiver in the green-veined mist ahead. Then they resolved. Three of them. They were men, or had been. Their forms were sketched in salt and seawater, translucent but sharp-edged. They wore oilskins that dripped a perpetual, silent stream. Their faces were hollows, but in those hollows burned points of cold, blue light. They carried the ghost-lights of their lanterns in one hand, and in the other, the shimmering outlines of flensing knives.

They didn’t speak. They simply turned their light-pitted faces towards us and began to walk. Their movement was wrong. They didn’t lift their feet. They flowed, as if the ground were water and they were the current.

“Back,” Elara hissed, her own knife—real, steel, comforting—in her hand.

We retreated. The path behind us was gone. The mist had closed in, a soft, impassable wall. The memory of the sea gives you ghosts, and the ghosts give you corners.

They spread out. Not with strategy, but with the instinct of predators who had done this before, in life and now in death. The one in the centre flowed faster, its knife held low. I could smell it now—ozone and rot and the iron-tang of old blood.

My mind went blank and white, like a sun-bleached bone. I fumbled for the key, my fingers clumsy. Elara met the centre ghost, her steel passing through its chest with a sound like tearing parchment. It didn’t slow. It swept its spectral knife towards her middle. She twisted, but the edge caught her coat, and the fabric frosted over, brittle and white with salt-crystal.

The other two were on me. The cold they radiated was a physical force, seizing my lungs. I yanked the key from my pocket. It flared.

Not a beam, not a weapon. A pulse. A single, clear note of golden light that rang out through the grey-green gloom. It was the sound of a door unlocking, of a seal breaking.

The ghosts recoiled. The light wasn’t fire; it didn’t burn them. It defined them. In that sudden, shocking radiance, I didn’t see monsters. I saw three men. I saw the net of scars on one’s knuckles, the missing tooth in another’s grimace, the desperate fear in all their eyes. They were memories, trapped in the act of violence. The light held them, suspended them in that truth for a heartbeat.

Then, with a sigh like a wave pulling back over shingle, they unravelled. The salt of them fell as a fine, grey dust. The cold light in their sockets guttered out.

The silence rushed back in, deeper than before. Elara was breathing hard, clutching her frost-stiffened coat. She stared at the key in my hand, its light fading back to that inner, sun-warm hum.

“It remembers them,” I said, my voice shaking. “It didn’t destroy them. It just… remembered them whole.”

Elara looked at the dust on the path. “A kinder fate than they deserved, likely.” But her eyes were on the key, and there was a new wariness in them. Not of the thing itself, but of what it revealed. It was a mirror, and not everything that looks into a mirror likes what it sees.

We found the skiff where the path of memory finally bled into something resembling water—a flat, mercury-coloured expanse that reflected nothing. The boat was a low, black shape, all tarred wood and cunning joints, looking less built than whittled by wind and current. A man sat on its gunwale, mending a net with fingers that moved like independent, clever animals. He didn’t look up as we approached.

This was Kael. He was built of the same stuff as his boat: weathered, laconic, all function. His hair was the grey of winter driftwood, his face a topography of seams and scars. His eyes, when he finally raised them, were the colour of the dull water around us—a flat, assessing grey.

“Lost,” he stated. It wasn’t a question.

“Looking for passage,” Elara said, her voice slipping into a trader’s cadence. “To the Heart.”

Kael’s fingers didn’t pause in their work. “No such place.”

“The central isle,” I said. “Where the currents gather.”

He glanced at me, then back to his net. “Aye. There’s a place. Doesn’t have a name. Doesn’t need one. Nothing there to trade for, girl. Just rocks and regret.”

“We’re not trading,” I said.

“Everyone’s trading.” He pulled a knot tight. “You trade comfort for truth, safety for knowledge, life for purpose. You’re here. So you’ve already started paying.” He nodded towards the horizon, where a permanent bank of fog brooded. “Your coin’s no good with me. I need silver. Or secrets.”

Elara placed a small, heavy pouch on the jetty beside him. It clinked with the solid, sleepy sound of real silver. Kael didn’t touch it. He looked at me.

“Heard a story,” he said, his voice a low rasp. “About a girl from the dryside who woke the old music. That you?”

The air felt thin. I nodded, once.

He considered me, his gaze a physical weight. “Then you have a secret. And I’ll take that for fare. One truth, girl. Why are you really here?”

It was a thief’s question. A smuggler’s question. He wasn’t asking for a cause. He was asking for the crack in the wall, the hidden compartment. I thought of my mother’s choice, of the wall on the Shingle Bank, of the key in my pocket that remembered ghosts into peace.

“I’m here,” I said, the words leaving me like a confession, “to find out if the thing that called my mother was a siren song… or a lighthouse.”

Kael was silent for a long moment. Then he gave a short, sharp nod, scooped up the pouch of silver, and stood. “Get in. Don’t touch the lines. Don’t speak to the water.”

Kael’s skiff moved through the mercury channels not by sail or oar, but by a kind of sympathetic magic. He had a series of worn, bone-white pegs along the gunwale, and he’d tap them with a mallet made of a sealed, resonant wood. Each tap sent a shiver through the boat and a corresponding ripple through the flat water, pushing us forward in a series of silent, gliding surges. It was like watching a stone skip itself.

He spoke in fragments, tossed over his shoulder like scraps to gulls.

“Morvan,” he said, when the silence had stretched into miles. “You seek his shadow.”

“We seek to stop what he’s begun,” Elara corrected, her eyes scanning the featureless banks of mist.

Kael barked a laugh, a dry, soundless thing. “Stop the tide. Good luck.” He tapped a peg, and we slid around a bend where the water turned the colour of tarnished brass. “He was one of you. Once.”

“One of what?” I asked.

“A Keybearer. Keeper of a Door. His was the Door of Tides, they say. Knew the rhythm of it all. The in, the out, the between.” Kael’s tapping took on a darker, more urgent rhythm as the water grew choppy. “But knowing a thing’s rhythm ain’t the same as respecting it. He thought the Veil’s heart—that central isle—was a lock. And he had the key. Thought if he turned it just so, he could still the currents. Own the silence between the waves. Make himself master of the memory of the sea.”

The ambition of it was so vast it was empty, like a scream in a cavern. To control not the sea, but its echo. Not the event, but the remembrance of it.

“What happened?” Elara’s voice was tight.

“The heart,” Kael said, his voice dropping, “isn’t a lock. It’s a wound. A place where all the memories bleed together. Raw. Unformed. He turned his key in it. Didn’t master it. He… agitated it. The memories got confused. Angry. The ghosts you met? They’re the least of it. He tore the fabric here. Things that should stay buried started remembering they were alive. Things that were never alive started dreaming they were. He didn’t become master. He became a symptom. The fever in the wound.”

I felt the key in my pocket, its warm hum. It was a piece of that same power. A tool for doors, for thresholds. Could it be used to mend a tear? Or would it only make a bigger one?

“Why does he want the other keys?” I asked.

Kael was quiet for so long I thought he hadn’t heard. The mist began to solidify into shapes ahead—the suggestion of rickety piers, of rooftops sagging under the weight of perpetual damp.

“A man who tears a wound,” he said finally, “usually thinks the only fix is to tear it wider. Maybe he thinks all the keys together will let him re-weave the fabric to his liking. Or maybe he just wants to be sure no one else can stitch it up.” He nodded towards the emerging settlement. “Trading post. We need supplies. You stay close, you stay quiet. This is the Flotsam Market. It trades in things that have been lost, and things that want to be.”

The Flotsam Market was a memory of a port. The buildings were made of shipwreck timber and caulked with despair. Everything was damp. The air smelled of wet wool, rotting fish, and a sweet, sickly undertone of ambergris and forgetfulness. Stalls sold curious things: bottles of captured laughter that sparkled murkily, compasses that pointed only to what you regretted, lenses that made the past in the air behind a person briefly visible—a shimmer of old joys and sorrows trailing them like a bridal train.

We stuck close to Kael, a dark, purposeful nucleus in the aimless, shuffling crowd. The people here had the look of those who had been soaked too long in memory; their features were blurred, their eyes distant, as if listening to a song only they could hear.

We were at a stall selling dried, luminous kelp when the ambush came.

It wasn’t with a shout. It was with a cessation. The murmur of the market died. The crowd parted, not in fear, but with a slow, viscous indifference, as if making way for the inevitable.

Three figures blocked the narrow alley leading back to the pier. They weren’t ghosts. They were living men, but men steeped in the Veil’s essence. Their skin had a waxy, waterlogged pallor. Their eyes were fully black, like a shark’s. They wore the livery of no house I knew—patched grey and green, the colour of deep water and rotting weed.

Morvan’s.

Kael didn’t hesitate. He threw the sack of supplies he’d just bought into the face of the first and drew a long, cruel-looking hook from his belt. “The pier!” he snarled at us.

Elara was already moving, her knife a silver flicker. She engaged the second man. Their clash was shockingly loud in the new silence.

The third came for me. He moved with a liquid grace, a dagger in his hand that wept a dark, oily fluid. I stumbled back, my hand closing around the key. But the market was full of living, semi-living things. Would the light act as a beacon? Would it make me a target for everything else here that remembered hunger?

I didn’t use it. I ducked under a swipe of the oily dagger, feeling the cold wind of its passage. I grabbed a handful of the luminous kelp from the stall and flung it at his face. It stuck, glowing, blinding him for a second. I ran.

Not towards the pier. Away. Into the labyrinth of the market. I heard Elara shout my name, the sound swallowed by the closing crowd.

I ran until my lungs burned with the damp air, ducking under stalls, through hanging sheets of salted fish, past mirrors that showed me glimpses of my own past—a child on the shingle, a mother’s smile, a wall of grey stone falling. The memories were a distraction, a drag on my soul.

I found myself in a dead-end courtyard, heaped with broken barrels and the skeletons of old crates. The sound of the market was a distant murmur. I was alone. The key was hot in my hand, almost vibrating. Not with fear. With awareness.

I leaned against a wet wall, trying to quiet my breathing. This was it. The separation. The test. Kael had spoken of trading—comfort for truth, safety for knowledge. I had traded my safety. Now I needed knowledge.

I held the key up. Not to use it. To listen to it.

In the absolute quiet of the dead end, I let my mind go still. I stopped fighting the pressure of the place, the weight of all its memories. I thought of my mother not as a mystery, but as a woman who heard a call. I thought of the music as a thread. I thought of the key as a needle.

The hum in my hand changed. It deepened, resonating with something beneath. Not the stone of the courtyard, but the memory in the stone. I saw, in my mind’s eye, not a map, but a pattern. A web of echoes. I could feel the strong, dark pulse of Morvan’s tear at the centre. I could feel the fainter, warmer pulse of Elara’s life, back towards the pier. And I could feel… a path. A thread of quieter memory, a forgotten alleyway used by long-dead lovers and secret messengers. A back way to the water.

It wasn’t sight. It was recognition. The key knew the architecture of this place because this place was made of the same stuff as doors—thresholds, crossings, secrets kept and passages made.

I wasn’t a fighter. I wasn’t a smuggler. I was the daughter of the woman with the laugh. I was a listener. And here, memory was the only compass that mattered.

I pushed off from the wall, the key held before me like a dowsing rod for time. I stepped towards a solid-looking wall of mossy brick. As I approached, the key’s hum peaked. The wall didn’t disappear. It simply… remembered being an archway, long ago. And for me, in that moment, it was one.

I walked through, into the damp, forgotten dark of the passage, following the thread of silent memory. I was trading my ignorance for skill. My fear for a direction. The price was being alone in the dark with all the ghosts of yesterday.

But I was learning. In the Veil, you don’t find your way. You remember it.

---

 Chapter 5

 CHAPTER 5

The memory of the sea was a wound in the world. I knew it before I saw it. The air in the passage changed, the salt-thief retreating before a new, more violent claimant. This salt didn’t powder; it screamed. It was the taste of a thing shattered, ground fine and left to scream its components into a static wind. It was the memory of betrayal, and like all potent memories, it had teeth.

I emerged from the archway’s remembered shadow into a light that had no source. It was the grey-white of a blank page, of a fog that had forgotten how to be wet. The ground under my boots was not stone, nor earth, but a coarse, granular substance that shifted with a sound like a sigh. The Shattered Wastes. Before me, the world didn’t end so much as it gave up. Land and sky and the idea of distance had been taken apart and reassembled by a mad, grieving hand.

Elara and Kael were waiting beside a spire of what looked like frozen glass, but which hummed with a low, grieving note. Elara’s face was tight, the warmth leached out by the ambient chill of the place. Kael stood as if planted, his usual fluid watchfulness gone rigid. The Wastes pressed on them, too.

“You’re late,” Kael said, his voice stripped of its usual laconic edge. It was just fact, hard and dry.

“The wall took time to remember,” I said, my own voice sounding thin. I held up the key. It was no longer humming, but vibrating at a pitch so high it felt like a tension in my teeth. It remembered this place, too.

Elara’s eyes tracked from the key to my face. She didn’t ask if I was followed. The Wasks would have swallowed any follower whole. “You feel it,” she said, not a question. “The fragmentation.”

I nodded. It wasn’t a feeling in my gut. It was a feeling in my mind. Like trying to hold a melody while someone sang a different tune in each ear. “It’s all… competing.”

“That’s the nature of a betrayal this deep,” Kael said, his gaze scanning the formless horizon. “Morvan didn’t just break a pact here. He broke a story. And stories are what hold places together. What’s left are the pieces, each holding its own version of the truth. None of them agree.”

Elara gestured toward the expanse. “The Wastes are not a geography. They are a consensus that failed. To cross, you must hold your own story firm. Let the fragments pull at you, and you’ll come apart. Your memory of yourself will scatter, and you’ll become just another echo, arguing with the wind.”

I looked out at the shifting, pearlescent gloom. Islands of clarity floated in the haze: a staircase leading to a door that opened into nothing; a grove of trees made of black coral, weeping salt tears; a courtyard, perfect and still, around which the fractured reality swirled like a slow storm. Each was a memory, intact and isolated. The spaces between them were the arguments.

“And the gate?” I asked.

“On the other side,” Kael said. “Guarded by the memory of what was lost. The strongest fragment of all.”

We began to walk. The granular sand gave way, in patches, to surfaces that were wrong. One moment my boot would crunch on what felt like crushed shell, the next it would meet a resistance like cold pudding, then a firmness like aged wood. The path was not a path. It was a suggestion, and we had to agree with it, step by step.

The first trial was not a monster. It was a melody.

It came from a floating shard of memory to our left—a fragment of a sunlit pier, the wood warm and whole. A tune whistled on it, jaunty and familiar. My father’s fishing song. The one he’d hum while mending nets, his big hands surprisingly deft with the needle and twine. A wave of homesickness, thick and sweet as syrup, washed over me. I could smell tar and fish and the oatcakes he’d bake on the griddle. My step faltered, turning toward it.

“Alex.” Elara’s voice was a hook in my collar. “That is not your memory.”

“It is,” I whispered, the longing a physical ache. “It’s his song.”

“It’s a memory of a song,” Kael corrected, his voice grim. “The Wastes have eaten a thousand fishermen. It’s offering you a composite. A lure. Step onto that pier, and you’ll spend eternity remembering a father who isn’t yours, mending nets that don’t exist.”

I forced my eyes away. The melody twisted, then, into something mournful, then into a shanty I didn’t know, then into a wordless keen. The fragment darkened, the sunny pier becoming rain-slicked and treacherous. I held my own story firm: my father, alive, his song his own, his hands his own. The fragment slid away, receding into the grey.

The second trial was a face.

It rose from a pool of what looked like still water, but reflected the blank sky instead of us. A woman’s face, formed of mist and regret. She had my mother’s eyes. The same sea-green, the same laugh lines at the corners. But the set of the mouth was wrong—bitter, where my mother’s was wry. The face spoke, and the voice was a rustle of reeds.

“You look for her? She left you. She heard a sweeter song and followed it. A child was an anchor. She cut the line.”

The words were stones dropped into my stomach. They resonated with the old, secret fear I’d scrubbed into flagstones, the one I’d never let surface. That her calling was just a prettier word for abandonment. That the music was more important than me.

“She loved me,” I said, but my voice was the whisper of the salt-thief.

“Did she?” the face sighed, beginning to blur. “Or did she love the idea of a daughter waiting? A fixed point to make her wandering seem romantic, instead of selfish?”

Elara’s hand found my arm, her grip fierce. “This is Morvan’s voice,” she hissed. “This is the poison he used. To make love seem like a cage, and betrayal seem like freedom. Do not let his memory define yours.”

I thought of my mother’s laugh. Not the idea of it, but the specific, unruly sound of it, bursting out of her at the market when a gull stole a vendor’s fish. I thought of the weight of her hand on my head, the smell of her skin—salt and lavender and ink. Not a symbol. A person. Flawed, called, but hers. My memory.

“You’re an echo,” I said to the face, my voice finding its grain. “And you’re out of tune.”

The face dissolved with a hiss, the pool of not-water swallowing itself.

We walked deeper. The pressure increased, a psychic weight that made my skull feel thin. The key in my hand was a live thing, reacting to every fragment we passed. It would grow warm near a memory of a locked chest, vibrate near the ghost of a threshold. It was learning the Wastes, mapping the fractures.

Kael stopped us before a wide, flat plain of mirror-smooth obsidian. It reflected the chaos above, making it seem as if we stood between two collapsing heavens. “This is the heart of it,” he said. “The memory of the moment of betrayal. It’s not a scene. It’s a… concussion.”

“How do we cross?” I asked. The mirror-surface didn’t look solid. It looked like a skin over an infinite drop.

“You don’t cross it,” Elara said softly. “You withstand it. The memory will play. We will be inside it. Hold onto your own truth. No matter what you see.”

We stepped onto the obsidian. It held.

The world snapped.

One moment, the formless grey. The next, I stood on the deck of a ship, but a ship like none I’d ever seen. Its hull was not wood, but something like mother-of-pearl, glowing with a soft, internal light. The sails were woven from twilight. The sea around us was not water, but liquid starfall, swirling with constellations. This was the Sea of Lyrion, whole.

Two figures stood at the bow. Morvan, but not as I’d seen him in the tear. This Morvan’s face was alive with fervour, his eyes bright with the reflected starlight. He was speaking, his voice passionate, weaving words in the air that seemed to take shape—bridges of light, towers of song.

Beside him was a woman. Elara, but younger. Centuries younger. Her hair was not silver-streaked, but a fall of pure night, and in her eyes was not weariness, but a terrible, hopeful love. She watched Morvan as if he were the axis of the world.

“It can be united,” Morvan was saying, his hand sweeping across the glorious sea. “The Veil, the Waking World, Lyrion… they are not separate realms. They are layers of the same song, played out of phase. We can be the conductors, Elara. We can harmonize them. No more separation. No more loneliness for those who hear the music. They can belong.”

The memory was not silent. I could feel the pull of his vision. It was intoxicating. It was the answer to every lonely listener’s prayer. It wasn’t power he was selling; it was belonging. The ultimate belonging.

“The cost,” Young Elara said, but her voice was weak with want. “The treaties… the boundaries exist for a reason.”

“The reason is fear!” Morvan turned to her, taking her hands. His touch was electric; I could feel its echo in the air. “Fear of the deep music, fear of true communion. We can build a new reason. A reason of unity. I have found the way. A single, perfect note, struck at the confluence of all realms. It will be a key to reshape reality itself.”

I knew, with a sickening lurch, what he meant. The key in my hand, the one that hummed and remembered, was not a copy. It was the consequence. This was the dream that had forged it. A beautiful, catastrophic dream.

The scene shifted. We were no longer on the ship. We were in a place that was all confluence—a cavern where roots of giant trees plunged into a sea of stars, where a river of liquid moonlight cut through stone from the Waking World. The heart of the Veil. Morvan stood at a nexus of leylines, his arms raised. A circle of others—listeners, seers, dreamers from all realms—stood with him, Elara at his right hand. Their faces were rapt.

He began to sing. It was not a song with words. It was the root of song. The vibration before sound. The air crystallized around it. The leylines brightened, converging on him. He was drawing the music of all places, all times, into himself. To forge his perfect note.

I could feel the terrible strain. The beauty curdled into hunger. He was not conducting. He was consuming. The light from the other listeners began to dim, flicker, as he pulled the music from them, channeling it toward his purpose. Elara’s face changed first—the hope cracking, revealing horror beneath. She tried to pull back, but the song had her. It had them all.

“Morvan, stop!” she cried, but her voice was a thread in a gale.

He didn’t stop. His eyes were no longer bright with vision, but blazing with a white, greedy fire. The perfect note was not about unity. It was about control. The ultimate control. To make all of reality remember his tune.

The confluence rebelled. The realms, forced into a harmony they did not choose, shuddered. The memory fractured around us—flashes of the tree-roots snapping, the star-sea boiling, the river of moonlight shattering into a million glassy shards. The listeners screamed as the feedback of the broken song tore through them, scouring their identities, leaving only raw, screaming echoes.

Morvan, at the centre, took the full force. But he did not shatter. He grasped. In the last instant before the collapse, he used the stolen music, the stolen wills of his followers, to forge an object. A focus. A key. It appeared in his hands, burning with stolen light. And with a final, wrenching act of theft, he used it not to heal the break, but to channel the catastrophic energy outward.

Away from himself.

Toward his followers.

Toward Elara.

The blast of fragmented reality hit her. I saw it happen. It did not kill her. It unmade the centuries around her. It scarred her with the raw memory of that betrayal, aging her not in body, but in spirit, in an instant. It cast her and the other survivors out, while Morvan, clutching his key, anchored himself in the suddenly-dead nexus.

The vision ended.

I was on my knees on the obsidian plain, vomiting dry air. The taste of stolen music and betrayal was a metal flood in my mouth. Elara stood beside me, rigid, her face a mask of ancient pain relived. Kael had a hand on her shoulder, his own jaw clenched so tight I heard the creak.

“He didn’t just want power,” I gasped, the understanding like a wound. “He wanted to be the only memory that mattered. To make the whole world remember his story.”

Elara nodded, a single, stiff movement. “And when the world refused, he broke it. He made the Wastes as a monument to his failure. And he kept the key.” She looked at the one in my hand. “That is a copy. But it is a memory of the original. It contains the same ambition. The same hunger. It will try to remember its purpose.”

I stood, my legs trembling. The key was cold now. Colder than the Wastes. I understood its hum, its knowing. It was a seed of that catastrophic unity. A little fragment of Morvan’s will.

We crossed the rest of the obsidian plain in silence. The weight of what we carried was no longer just physical. It was historical. It was moral.

The gate was not what I expected. It wasn’t grand. It was a tear. A vertical rip in the fabric of the Wastes, about ten feet high and shimmering with a unstable, oily light. It looked like a wound that refused to heal. Before it stood the guardian.

It was not a creature. It was an echo, given form. It was the composite memory of all the listeners Morvan had betrayed. It shifted as we approached—one moment a man with a harp of broken strings, the next a woman with eyes of silent music, the next a child holding a shell to a ear that was no longer there. Its form was a chorus of absence.

“You carry the shadow of the key,” it spoke, its voice a multitude of whispers, sad and angry. “Why should the broken let the breaker’s tool pass?”

Elara stepped forward. “I am Elara. I was there. I am broken, too.”

The guardian solidified, taking her younger form—the one from the memory, full of hope. “You lived. You carry the scar, but you live. Our music is gone. Our stories are gone. We are the argument left behind.”

“I live to argue back,” Elara said, her voice fierce with a grief as deep as the sea. “This one,” she gestured to me, “carries the key, but not the will. She is a listener. She hears the fragments. She can remember you. Not as he made you—victims, echoes. But as you were. Individuals. Songs.”

The guardian wavered. It was a temptation, I realized. To be remembered truly, even by one person, was a kind of power against the oblivion of the Wastes.

“What is your name?” I asked, the question leaving me before I thought.

The shifting forms stilled. For a moment, it settled into the shape of an old man with a deeply lined face and hands calloused from rope. “Caden,” the whisper came, singular and surprised. “I… I was a net-mender. From the coastal hamlet of Brinewatch. I heard the tides in the knots.”

“Caden,” I repeated, and I poured my listening into it. Not to his death, not to his betrayal. But to the man who heard music in practical things. The memory solidified, grew details—the smell of tar, the way he’d whistle through his teeth, the particular knot he used to secure a float. I didn’t invent them. I pulled them from the echo’s own fading truth.

The guardian—Caden—let out a sound like a sob of relief. For a second, he was whole, distinct. Then he began to fragment again, but slower. “A kindness,” the many whispers returned. “A pinprick of light. It is not enough. But it is something.” The form gestured to the tear. “The gate is unstable. It remembers being a doorway, but also a rupture. The key may steady it. Or it may widen the tear and unmake this place, and you with it. That is the trial. Do you trust the memory of a tool made for breaking to remember how to mend?”

I looked at the key. I knew its hunger now. I knew its origin story was a lie written in stolen music. But I also knew what Gran said: the salt was the memory of the sea, trying to get back in. This key was a memory of a perfect, terrible note, trying to find its lock. Maybe it didn’t want to break. Maybe it just wanted to be. To fulfill its purpose, however warped.

But I was not Morvan. My purpose was not unity through control. It was navigation through listening. I didn’t need to play the note. I just needed to remind the door what it was.

I approached the shimmering tear. The key blazed to life, not with heat, but with a frantic, eager vibration. It remembered this. It wanted to connect, to complete the circuit. I could feel its pull toward the centre of the tear, to strike that world-reshaping note.

I closed my eyes. I thought not of the key’ purpose, but of the gate’s. Before the betrayal, this was not a wound. It was a passage. A threshold between the Veil and the shores of Lyrion. A place of exchange, of wonder. I listened past the scream of the rupture, for the memory of the threshold. The sigh of a traveller stepping from one world to another. The whisper of starlight on seawater. The solid, welcoming feel of a lintel under a pilgrim’s hand.

I fed those memories to the key. Not to use it, but to orient it. To remind it that doors are for passing through, not for breaking down.

“You are a key,” I whispered to it. “And this is a door. Remember.”

I raised it, not in a striking motion, but in offering. I touched its cold metal to the edge of the shimmering tear.

The effect was instantaneous and violent. The oily light convulsed. For a terrifying second, the tear widened, and a howl of pure entropy blasted out. The key throbbed with malevolent joy. It was remembering its original function—to break, to consume.

But I held on. I poured my own memory into my grip. The memory of my mother’s hand, guiding mine on a latch. The memory of my own front door, solid against the salt-wind. The memory of the archway in the alley, remembering itself for me. The simple, profound truth of a threshold: it exists to be crossed, not to be owned.

The key’s vibration changed. The hunger softened, confused by this quieter, older memory of purpose. The tear shuddered. The edges began to… knit. Not with flesh, but with remembered stone, with arching coral, with the ghost of an inscription in a language of waves. The light within it calmed, shifting from unstable oil-slick to the deep, placid blue of twilight sea.

A passage formed. On the other side, I saw not chaos, but a shoreline of black sand, and beyond it, a sea of liquid stars, gentle now. The air that sighed through carried a scent I’d never known but recognized in my blood: salt, but not the thief’s salt. The salt of a living, endless sea.

The gate was open.

The guardian, now a shifting, softer constellation of echoes, bowed its many heads. “You remembered a door,” it whispered. “A finer trick than forging a key.”

Elara exhaled, a sound of release held for centuries. Kael just nodded, a new, assessing look in his eyes as he gazed from the mended gate to me.

I stepped back, the key now quiet, almost dormant, in my hand. It felt different. Not tamed. But… redirected. Its memory had been edited, if only for a moment.

We passed through the gate. The transition was not a step, but a sigh. The screaming silence of the Wastes was replaced by the vast, rhythmic hush of the sea. The air was clean, charged, ancient. We stood on the shore of Lyrion.

I looked back. The gate was a perfect arch of pearlescent coral now, framing the chaotic grey of the Wastes like a window into a storm. A boundary. Not a wound.

I had done it. I had traded my ignorance for skill. My fear for a direction.

But as I turned to face the starry sea, I saw the hook that would pull us—pull me—into the next chapter of this.

Far down the black-sand beach, where the star-light met the water, a single, familiar figure stood waiting. He wore simple clothes, his hands empty, his posture relaxed. He looked like a man come to watch the tide.

It was Morvan.

And he was smiling, not with triumph, but with the warm, terrifying welcome of a host who has been expecting you, and who is very, very pleased you used his key.

---

 Chapter 6

 CHAPTER 6

The salt here was different. It didn’t steal; it bestowed. It lay on the black sand like a dusting of crushed diamonds, and the air it seasoned was thick and vital, filling the lungs like a second, older blood. It was the salt Gran had dreamed of—the memory of the sea, not its revenge. For a single, suspended breath, I believed we had outrun everything. The Wastes, the key’s hunger, the long, dry shadow of what we’d left behind.

Morvan’s smile was the hook. It set deep, past bone, into the soft tissue of certainty. He didn’t move. He simply stood there, a man at the edge of a starry tide, his welcome a net woven from our own arrival.

Kael’s hand went to the hilt of his blade, a motion as instinctive as a breath. “A reception,” he said, his voice flat. “How courteous.”

Elara took a half-step forward, her body a tense line between us and the distant figure. “He is not alone.”

She saw it in the way the starlight bent, in the slight, oily shimmer in the air around him. I felt it a moment later—a pressure, not against my skin, but against the memory of it. The key in my hand, now cool and placid, gave a single, dull throb. A reminder of provenance.

Morvan raised a hand, not in threat, but in a gesture of benevolent halt, as if we were children about to bolt into a street. Then he turned and walked, not toward us, but along the shoreline, vanishing behind a jagged spine of obsidian that speared up from the sand.

“He’s herding us,” Kael muttered.

“He is showing us the path,” Elara corrected, though her tone held no comfort. “The choice to follow is the illusion he is generous enough to grant.”

We followed. The black sand whispered underfoot, each grain holding a pinprick of the sea’s light. The starry water lapped with a sound like a million slow, shattering crystals. It was beautiful, and the beauty was a kind of violence, because it demanded you forget why you had come. I focused on the back of Kael’s worn coat, on the set of Elara’s shoulders. I focused on the key, my thief, now so unnervingly quiet.

The obsidian ridge formed a natural corridor, its walls reflecting the star-sea in distorted, liquid streaks. At its end, the path opened onto a wider crescent of beach. And there, before a second, smaller arch of coral—a sibling to the gate we’d mended—stood three figures.

Morvan was not among them.

The one in the centre was a woman. She had the same polished, ageless look as Elara, but where Elara’s severity was that of a honed blade, this woman’s was that of a closed door. Her hair was the colour of cold ash, bound tight. Her eyes were the grey of a winter harbour. She wore a practical, dark-grey tunic and trousers, unadorned. Her hands were empty.

“Kael,” she said. Her voice was calm, clear, and carried over the gentle hush of the waves without effort. “The Master sends his congratulations. Your navigation was… admirably persistent.”

Kael stopped. He didn’t draw his blade. He became very still, the way a rock is still before the tide rushes around it. “Lira.”

“You are to come with us,” Lira said. It wasn’t a command; it was a recitation of fact. “The artefact you carry is unstable. The Master would see it secured. The girl and the Warden are irrelevant to this transaction.”

Elara made a low sound in her throat. I felt the key warm, not with anger, but with a sudden, sharp attention. It remembered being called an artefact.

“Transaction,” Kael echoed. He shifted his weight, just slightly. I saw the calculation in the line of his jaw. He was measuring the distance to the new coral arch, the placement of the two silent, broad-shouldered men flanking Lira. “He always did have a merchant’s soul. What’s the price this time?”

“Your compliance,” Lira said, “for their continued passage. A simple trade. Refuse, and the terms become more complex.” Her winter-harbour eyes flicked to me, then to Elara. “And more final.”

The air changed. The benevolent salt turned sharp. Kael’s stillness broke into motion.

He didn’t go for Lira. He lunged to the left, toward the taller of the two men, his blade a sudden, singing arc in the star-light. It was a feint, brutal and convincing. The man parried, the clash of steel a shocking, vulgar sound against the sea’s hymn. The second man moved to flank.

“Run!” Kael barked, the word ripped from him. “The arch! Now!”

He was creating a chaos only we could use. A distraction written in violence. Elara was already moving, her hand closing around my wrist. “He buys seconds,” she hissed. “Spend them wisely.”

We ran. Not toward the arch Lira guarded, but back the way we’d come, toward the main gate. Our boots tore the perfect black sand. I heard another clash, a grunt of pain—I didn’t know whose. The key was hot in my hand now, pulsing in time with my hammering heart.

We burst from the obsidian corridor onto the main shore. The pearlescent arch of the mended gate stood a hundred yards away, a window onto the grey desolation of the Wastes. Freedom. A retreat.

A figure stepped from the shadow of the arch.

It was one of Lira’s men. He must have been posted there from the start. He held a short, heavy blade, and his expression was one of bored proficiency.

Elara skidded to a halt, pushing me behind her. “Through the gate,” she said, her voice low and urgent. “Do not stop.”

“I’m not leaving you—”

“You are following an order,” she cut in, and for the first time, I heard the Warden in her, absolute and unyielding. “The key is the priority. Go.”

The man advanced. Elara met him. She had no weapon but her hands, her knowledge, the weight of her long memory. She moved like water finding a crack, flowing inside his guard, her strike aimed not to maim, but to disrupt, to unbalance. He was stronger, heavier. He backhanded her with the pommel of his blade. She took the blow on her forearm with a sickening crunch, but used the momentum to spin, her foot sweeping his legs.

He went down, hard. Elara looked at me, her face pale, one arm hanging useless. “Now, Alex!”

I ran for the gate. The star-sea’s hush roared in my ears. I was ten paces away. Five.

A hand grabbed my cloak from behind, yanking me off my feet. I landed on my back in the sand, the breath punched from me. Lira stood over me, her grey tunic barely ruffled. She held a thin, cruel-looking dagger. Behind her, I saw the other man dragging a limp Kael toward the smaller arch. Bought seconds, spent.

“The key,” Lira said, kneeling, her voice still terribly calm. “You are not worthy of its memory.”

She reached for my hand. I clutched the key to my chest. It was burning now, a core of remembered forge-fire. Elara cried out—a raw, furious sound—and slammed into Lira, knocking her aside.

They fell together, a tangle of limbs and sharp edges on the black sand. Elara was fighting with one arm, with teeth, with desperate, forgotten grace. Lira’s dagger flashed.

Elara went rigid. Then soft.

She collapsed onto her side, a dark, swift stain spreading across the front of her tunic from just below her ribs. The star-dust on the sand drank it hungrily.

Lira stood up, wiping her dagger on her thigh. She looked from Elara’s still form to me. “The key,” she repeated, as if there had been no interruption.

Something broke in the world. Or in me. The careful, fearful dam I’d built inside my mind—the one that held back the key’s whispering, grasping memories—shattered.

I didn’t summon the power. I fell into it.

It was not a stream, but an ocean. The memory of pressure, of crushing depths. The memory of stone being born in fire and laid down, grain by grain, over millennia. The memory of a city’s foundations, not as rock, but as intention. The key in my hand was no longer a tool, but a conduit, and I was the ground it discharged into.

I saw Lira’s eyes widen, the bored proficiency replaced by a flicker of primal alarm. She lunged with the dagger.

I didn’t move. I let the memory rise.

The salt in the air around us crystallised. Not the gentle diamond-dust of the living sea, but the hard, white, thirsting salt of the Wastes. It snapped into existence around Lira, a cloud of biting grit, then a shell, then a sarcophagus. It happened in the space between heartbeats. One moment she was a woman in grey, the next she was a statue of white salt, her expression frozen in that final moment of understanding, dagger outstretched.

The memory released me. I dropped to my knees, retching, my body shaking as if with a fever. The world swam. The beautiful star-sea seemed to dim. I crawled to Elara.

Her eyes were open, staring at the strange sky. Her breath was a shallow, wet rasp. The stain on her tunic was vast. I pressed my hands against it, the warmth of her blood a shocking contrast to the cold sand. The key, now dull and heavy, fell from my grasp onto the sand beside her.

“Elara. Look at me.”

Her gaze drifted to mine. There was no fear in it. Only a profound, weary recognition. “You… remembered a different salt,” she whispered, a faint smile touching her bloodless lips. Then her eyes clouded, looking past me. “The bridge…”

Her body went slack. The rasping breath stopped.

The vast, rhythmic hush of the sea was the only sound. I knelt there, my hands red, the key lying in the black sand like a piece of discarded scrap. The salt statue of Lira stood a few feet away, a grotesque monument. Down the beach, the smaller arch was empty. Kael was gone.

I had done it. I had drawn deep on the key’s power. I had saved myself.

And I was utterly, completely alone.

The hook for the next chapter was not in the distance. It was in the weight of the still key, and in the cold, gathering realisation that the only path left now led toward the one who had wanted it opened. It was in the ghost of Elara’s last words.

The bridge.

I lifted my head. Beyond the mended gate, the Wastes lay grey and silent. But from the direction of the smaller arch, the one Lira had guarded, a new sound whispered on the salt-tinged air. Not the sea. It was the sound of wind moaning through a deep, unseen place. A conceptual abyss.

And I would have to cross it.

---

 Chapter 7

 CHAPTER 7

The salt came for me again. Not the slow, patient thief of home, but a swift, violent creditor. It had found its due in Lira, and now it sought a balance from the rest of us. It crusted the blood on my hands into a stiff, brown-red glaze. It powdered Elara’s still face, collecting in the hollow of her throat, filling the space between her parted lips. It was claiming her, grain by grain. The memory of the sea, Gran would say, trying to get back in. I saw it differently now. The sea didn’t want its memory back. It wanted to turn everything into a memory. A dry, white thought.

I don’t know how long I knelt. Time was the rasp of waves on black sand, nothing more. The key was a cold, dead weight beside my knee. I had no strength to touch it. The power that had surged through me was gone, leaving a hollowed-out ache, as if my bones were now empty conduits. I had saved myself. The phrase was a stone in my gut.

A shadow fell across the sand.

I didn’t look up. Let it be another guardian. Let it be the sea’s revenge, made manifest. The shadow lengthened, then resolved into two boots, worn soft at the heel, crusted with the same white filigree that patterned my kitchen floor. Kael.

He said nothing. He moved past me to Elara, his movements fluid and silent. He knelt, his back to me, and for a long moment he was still. Then his shoulders dropped, a minute surrender. He placed two fingers against the side of her neck. He waited. He did not look at the salt-statue of his sister. His focus was a physical thing, a pressure in the air.

He shifted, his hands sliding under her shoulders and knees. He lifted her as if she weighed no more than driftwood. Her head lolled against his chest. He turned and his eyes met mine. They were the colour of the Wastes under a winter sun, flat and without judgement.

“The skiff,” he said, his voice the scrape of stone on stone. “Now.”

“She’s gone.” My own voice was a stranger’s, cracked and salted.

“The body is a vessel,” he said, already walking toward the smaller arch, the one that led not to the Wastes, but to the moaning dark. “It can be refilled. But not here. The salt is too hungry here.”

He disappeared into the arch’s shadow. I was alone again with the monument and the key. The wind’s moan deepened, a sound that seemed to come from the throat of the earth itself. The bridge. I fumbled for the key. Its metal was no longer warm, no longer alive. It was just a thing. I shoved it into my pocket, the weight of it pulling at my coat. I stood, my legs trembling, and followed Kael into the dark.

The skiff was not a boat. It was a suggestion of one, carved from a single, curved piece of bone-white wood that glimmered with a faint inner light, like the memory of moonlight. It floated not on the water, but a hand’s breadth above a river of moving shadow that poured from the archway, flowing soundlessly into a vast, subterranean cavern. The air was cold and smelled of deep stone and damp roots. The moaning was the wind in countless unseen fissures above.

Kael laid Elara in the hull. He produced a flask from his belt, uncorked it, and tipped a few drops of clear liquid between her lips. He did it with a ritual care, massaging her throat until she swallowed by reflex. Then he took his place at the stern, one hand on a tiller that was just a polished branch.

“Get in.”

I clambered over the side. The skiff didn’t rock. It felt less like boarding a vessel and more like stepping onto a floating piece of the world’s skeleton. I crouched beside Elara. In the skiff’s phosphorescent glow, she looked worse. The salt had been wiped from her face, but her skin was the colour of ash. The blood on her tunic was a black continent.

Kael pushed off from the stone ledge. The skiff slid into the current of shadow, silent as a thought. The archway shrank behind us, a diminishing frame of grey light, and then we were in the belly of the earth. Stalactites hung like stone teeth above a river of nothing. The only light was our own, casting our shadows, huge and wavering, against the cavern walls.

“Where are we going?” My voice was swallowed by the immensity.

“Away from the Gate,” he said. “The power you unleashed… it will have drawn attention. Other things listen, in the deep places.”

“Is she alive?”

“A thread,” he said. “No more.”

“The water you gave her?”

“The last of Lyrion’s tears.” He looked at me then, his face all planes and shadows in the low light. “You broke the shell. You mended the gate. You used a power you do not understand to stop a woman you did not know. Why?”

The question hung in the cold air. I had no answer that would fit words. I saw Lira’s face, not in hatred, but in a desperate, shattered love. I felt the key’s awful hunger. I remembered the different salt—the salt of my own memory, of a kitchen floor and a grandmother’s voice—and how it had felt stronger, more real, than the killing salt of this place.

“She was going to kill me,” I said, lamely.

“She was,” Kael acknowledged. “And you turned her into a lesson. A monument to your own fear.” He returned his gaze to the dark river ahead. “My sister was a guardian. The last. She believed the gate must remain sealed, at any cost. Even the cost of her humanity. You have just proven her right.”

Shame, hot and acidic, rose in my throat. “I didn’t mean to—”

“Intent is a luxury for the powerless,” he cut in, his voice still devoid of anger. It was just a statement of fact. “You held the key. You acted. Now we live in the world your action made. In this world, my sister is salt, and the last Healer of Lyrion bleeds out on the floor of my skiff.”

The truth of it was a physical blow. I wrapped my arms around myself, the key in my pocket digging into my thigh. We travelled in silence for a time measured only by the slow beat of my own heart.
